Emily:

Mark’s actual exit, when it came, was almost insultingly small.

There was a call one Sunday afternoon while Ethan was at Maya’s. Claire stood at the sink with the tea towel twisted in her hands and listened to her husband say words like posted permanently, not fair on either of you, we’ve grown apart, as if their marriage were a shirt he’d accidentally outgrown. He mentioned a flat off-base “for now,” and somebody named Jenna once, too quickly, then corrected to “a friend from admin.”

A month later an envelope arrived with paperwork and a copy of a signed separation agreement. His house keys came back in a padded bag, label smudged by rain. He texted Ethan twice that term—generic check-ins that could have been sent to anyone’s son—and when Ethan answered with polite, careful sentences, the replies came slower each time.

By the time Claire said, “It’s just us now,” in the kitchen one evening, Ethan only shrugged and said, “It’s been just us for ages,” and went back to chopping carrots. He knew who his team were.

By the middle of the year, the changes were small enough to deny and big enough that no one really could.

Maya noticed them first, of course. It was in the job description she’d quietly written for herself: watch him; notice what he can’t say out loud yet.

At first it was just his skin.

One afternoon on the bus, she reached for his hand without thinking, to yank him back down when he overbalanced as they went over a speed hump. Her fingers closed around his wrist.

“Careful,” she said. “You nearly ate the floor.”

“Graceful as ever,” Zoe added dryly.

“Shut up,” Ethan muttered, but Maya was only half-listening. The inside of his wrist was smooth under her thumb, softer than it had been. Still warm, still solid, but the faint sandpapery texture she remembered from last year—when he’d had a phase of forgetting what moisturiser was—was gone.

She let go and watched, later, as he fiddled with his hoodie cuff. The way the fabric glided over his forearm, the way the light caught on the fine hairs there, made something in her cataloguing brain go, file under: different.

A few weeks after that, it was his hair.

She was sitting behind him in maths, doodling half-attentively in the margin of her notes, when she realised his hair brushed the top of his hoodie in a way it hadn’t a month ago. It curled slightly at the ends now, the weight finally catching up with the stubborn straightness. When he dropped his head to concentrate, it fell forward, obscuring his ears.

He should tie that back, she thought, absurdly. Or let me.

At lunch, she said, “You need a trim,” purely to see what he’d say.

He touched the back of his head self-consciously. “Mum says the same. She keeps saying ‘book it in’ and then getting swamped. It’s fine.”

“It’s past fine,” Zoe put in. “You’re two weeks away from ‘ethereal choirboy’.”

Maya imagined him with his hair tucked behind one ear, maybe a clip. The image made her smile and blush, and she hoped neither of them noticed.

The hips were harder to ignore.

Emma’s jeans—his jeans—had always been snug. That was half the point. But one evening at her place, when he bent to plug in his laptop, the denim pulled across him in a way that made her glance twice. Not lewd, not exaggerated. Just… curved, where once he’d been more or less straight down from the waist.

“You okay?” he asked, catching her stare when he straightened up.

“Yes,” she said too quickly. “I was—never mind. Do you want popcorn?”

Later, alone in her room, she stood in front of her own mirror and turned sideways, comparing silhouettes in her mind. Hers, his, Zoe’s. If you blurred the outlines a little in your imagination, Ethan’s slotting closer to theirs now than to the blocky shapes of the boys on the bus.

It wasn’t just her perception. The girls talked.

One afternoon at the back of the oval, while Ethan fetched forgotten water bottles and the boys argued about offside, Zoe nudged her.

“Have you noticed?” she said, too casually.

“Noticed what,” Maya asked, even though she knew.

“The way things fit him now,” Zoe said. “The jeans. That grey hoodie. The shirt we chickened out of buying.” Her eyes glittered. “Our little project is… progressing.”

Maya shot her a look. “He’s not a project.”

“He is and he isn’t,” Zoe said. “He’s a person we’re protecting. He’s also living proof that the world doesn’t end when you take your foot off the testosterone gas pedal.”

Maya’s stomach fluttered. “Claire said the doctor warned her it would be gradual. That it’s mostly about… slowing things down. Giving him time.”

“It is,” Zoe said. “Mostly.”

There was something in the way she said that—light, but with a metallic glint underneath—that made Maya frown.

“Zoe,” she said. “You haven’t… done anything… have you?”

Zoe popped a grape into her mouth. “Define ‘anything’.”

“Zoe.”

“Relax,” she said. “I’m not cooking up potions in my shed. I’m just… augmenting what’s already happening. He wants smoother. Calmer. Safer. I’m helping.”

“You can’t know that’s what he wants,” Maya said, but the protest felt weak even as she said it. Ethan had never once complained about his softer body, only about the way the boys reacted to it.

Zoe’s gaze softened, just a fraction. “He shows you what he wants,” she said. “He keeps coming back. To your kitchen. To my ridiculous rituals. To the mall. If he truly hated any of it, he’d run.”

Maya thought of Ethan on the bus, of the way he had said, I don’t hate it, about that checked shirt, and then couldn’t stop touching his bare wrists where the cuffs had brushed.

“Still,” she said. “Don’t… push him too fast.”

“Oh, trust me,” Zoe said. “The world is way ahead of me on pushing. I’m just… counter-programming.”

She let it drop, but the unease stayed. Maya tucked it away beside all the other things she was still figuring out about Zoe: the way she alternated between vicious and tender, the way she watched Maya when she thought no one was looking.

The very slight breast development was the point of no return.

It wasn’t dramatic. No one else would have seen it under a T-shirt. But Maya did, because one weekend at her place, it was hot, and he’d peeled off his hoodie and sat cross-legged on her floor in a thin singlet while they revised.

She’d been explaining something about algebra, gesturing at his notebook, when she saw it: the faintest swell under the fabric, a softness at his chest where before there had been only flatness. Not enough to even call “buds” yet. Just a suggestion, a question mark.

Her words faltered.

“What?” he said. “Did I mess it up?”

“No,” she said, too quickly. “It’s fine. You’re fine.”

He gave her a puzzled look, then went back to his sums.

That night, in bed, Maya stared at the ceiling and let the realisation wash over her.

He was changing. Not just in style or habit, but in his body. And instead of recoiling, her heart had… doubled down. The boy she loved was being softened, tilted, nudged towards a shape the world would read differently. And every step seemed to make him more him.

She had no idea what to do with that except hold it carefully and promise herself she’d keep pace, wherever the road went.

If you’d asked Maya, a month before, whether she might ever kiss Zoe, she’d have laughed in your face.

She knew she liked girls, in a vague way. Knew she tripped over her words more around ones with sharp eyes and quick mouths. But she’d filed that under “future problem” and thrown all her immediate emotional energy into Ethan: his safety, his unfolding, the dizzy newness of being someone’s first real girlfriend.

Then came the afternoon in her bedroom when Zoe sat too close.

It was raining, the kind of persistent soft rain that turned the world grey and made the roof sing. Ethan had gone home early—Claire had texted that he was needed to help with dinner. Maya and Zoe had stayed to finish a group assignment, sprawled on Maya’s bed with laptops open and notebooks everywhere.

They’d actually worked for a solid hour. Then the wifi blipped, the document autosaved, and everything slowed.

Zoe closed her laptop with a sigh and stretched, T-shirt riding up a fraction at her waist.

“I swear,” she said, “if Mr Carter makes one more joke about ‘ladies and their organisation skills’ I’m switching his coffee for decaf.”

Maya laughed, rolling onto her back. “You’d be doing the universe a favour. He’s unbearable as it is.”

Silence settled, comfortable. Rain pattered. Somewhere in the house, Maya’s mum hummed along with the radio.

“You’re good at this, you know,” Zoe said after a moment.

“At what,” Maya asked.

“This,” Zoe said, gesturing vaguely around the room. “Holding a whole person in your head and caring about what happens to them. Without turning into a martyr or a doormat.”

“You mean Ethan?” Maya said.

“And your mum. And probably half your class, if we’re counting the way you keep checking on people when they look like they’re going to cry in the toilets.” Zoe’s mouth quirked. “But yes. Mainly Ethan.”

Maya stared at the water stains on her ceiling. She’d had that particular patch memorised since Year Seven.

“I love him,” she said simply. The word didn’t scare her. It sat warm and solid in her mouth. “I didn’t think it was possible to… feel that much about someone else’s breathing.”

Zoe snorted softly. “That’s grotesque and sweet.”

“It terrifies me,” Maya admitted. “Not him. The way the world is with people like him. Like us.”

“Us,” Zoe repeated. “You’re putting yourself in his category now?”

Maya shrugged, cheeks heating. “We’re all… bent a bit. Out of the default shape.”

There was a rustle as Zoe shifted closer. The mattress dipped. Maya could feel the warmth of her now, the faint citrus of her shampoo.

“Yeah,” Zoe said quietly. “We are.”

Maya turned her head. Zoe was propped on one elbow, looking down at her. Without her usual armour of sarcasm, her face looked younger. Softer, but no less intent.

“What,” Maya said, and the word came out more like a breath than a question.

“You look tired,” Zoe said. “In that way where your heart’s doing overtime.”

Maya huffed a small laugh. “Doctor’s orders now?”

“Observation,” Zoe said. “And… selfishness.”

Maya frowned. “Selfishness how?”

“Because,” Zoe said, and now her voice did something Maya hadn’t heard before—something like hesitation, “you’re pouring so much into him that I keep wanting to remind you that you’re… allowed other things. Other people.”

“Like?” Maya asked, even though the air between them was already fizzing with the answer.

“Like me,” Zoe said.

There it was. Simple and devastating.

Maya’s heart did a strange double-step. She stared up at her, cataloguing: the dark fringe half in her eyes, the freckle on her left cheek, the tension in her jaw like she was braced for a blow.

“I thought you…” Maya began, and then realised there wasn’t a tidy way to finish that sentence.

“Hated him?” Zoe supplied. “Wanted him out of the picture?”

Maya didn’t nod, but she didn’t deny it either.

“I was jealous,” Zoe said, far too calmly. “Am jealous. Of him. He gets your firsts. Your automatic. Your default worry. And he’s not even… my type.”

Maya blinked. “What is your type?”

“Difficult,” Zoe said, with a half-smile. “And female. And annoyingly kind without being stupid about it.”

Maya’s throat went dry. “Oh.”

“Yeah,” Zoe said. “Oh.”

Silence again. The rain filled it, hissing gently on the gutters.

“I’m not…” Maya said slowly. “I’m not… available. To date you.”

“I know,” Zoe said. “I’m not asking.”

“Then what are you—”

“I’m telling you,” Zoe said, and now there was steel under the softness, “that loving him doesn’t have to be the only shape you ever get. You’re allowed to want other things alongside it. And I…” Her voice dropped. “I want you. In whatever way doesn’t blow everything up.”

Maya’s chest hurt. “Zoe…”

“I’m not going to steal you,” Zoe said quickly. “I’m not that cliché. And I’m not going to use him to get to you; that would be gross, and you’d never forgive me. But I also… can’t keep pretending I only care because you care. I care because you’re… you.”

She reached out, very slowly, and brushed a strand of hair off Maya’s forehead. It was a touch softer than any she’d ever used on Ethan, less performative, more reverent.

Maya’s breath hitched. She hadn’t realised, until that exact moment, how badly she’d wanted to be seen like this. Not as “the good girl who looks after everyone,” but as someone someone else wanted. Directly. Fiercely.

“Can I…?” Zoe said, and for once she didn’t finish the sentence with a joke.

Maya’s answer surprised even her.

“Yes,” she whispered.

Zoe leaned down. The kiss was not cinematic. Their noses bumped; Maya’s hand, reaching up, smacked the edge of the laptop and sent it wobbling. Then their mouths found each other properly and the world narrowed to warm, soft pressure and the faint taste of apple from the juice Zoe had been drinking.

It was nothing like kissing Ethan. With him, there was a trembling sort of wonder, a carefulness, as if they were both afraid of breaking something fragile between them.

With Zoe, there was… recognition. A meeting of sharp edges. No one was leading; no one was being led. It was two currents colliding and then, somehow, flowing in the same direction.

Maya’s stomach swooped. Her hand fisted in the fabric of Zoe’s T-shirt. Zoe made a small sound against her mouth, half-surprised, half triumphant.

They broke apart at the same time, breathless for reasons that weren’t just physiological.

“Wow,” Zoe said softly. “Okay.”

Maya touched her own lips with the back of her hand, as if to check they were still there.

“That was…” She trailed off, because no single word fit.

“A mistake?” Zoe asked, but her eyes were hopeful in a way that made Maya’s heart twist.

“No,” Maya said immediately. “Not a mistake. Just… a lot.”

Zoe exhaled. “That it is.”

Guilt pricked at the edges of Maya’s mind, but it wasn’t aimed where she expected. She didn’t feel like she’d betrayed Ethan, exactly. It felt more like she’d crossed a border she hadn’t known existed and now had to figure out how to redraw the map.

“I love him,” she said again, because that still felt like a steadying anchor.

“I know,” Zoe said. “And I’m… weirdly okay with that.”

“You are?”

“I didn’t say I like it,” she said wryly. “But I like you. And I like that you love someone who isn’t going to grow up to be an arsehole. Makes me think you have taste.”

Maya laughed, shaky. “You’re impossible.”

“Yes,” Zoe said. “And so are you. And so is he. We’re a triangle, not a line. We’ll figure it out.”

Maya thought of Ethan, of the way his face lit up when she walked into a room. Of the way he listened. Of his changing body, that soft-framed silhouette that made something deep inside her settle.

Then she thought of Zoe, here, now, eyes still half-closed from their kiss, jaw still tense as if braced for rejection.

“My feelings for you feel… different,” Maya said slowly. “Not less. Just… sideways.”

Zoe nodded. “That tracks. I can work with sideways.”

“I don’t know what to do with any of it yet,” Maya admitted.

“You don’t have to,” Zoe said. “Not tonight. Tonight you can just… know it. That you are loved stupidly in multiple directions.”

Maya’s throat tightened. No one had ever phrased it like that before. Loved stupidly in multiple directions.

“Is this going to hurt him?” she asked, because that was the only question that mattered.

“It’ll only hurt him if we lie,” Zoe said. “Or if we use him. We’re not going to do either. Right?”

“Right,” Maya said, and meant it.

Zoe lay back down beside her, close but not touching this time, both of them staring up at the water stains on the ceiling.

Outside, the rain thickened, drumming its own steady beat.

In her chest, Maya felt something unfamiliar bloom: a sense of being tethered to two people at once, not torn between them but held, a weird, delicate balance.

She loved Ethan more than ever—his softness, his bravery, the way he was slowly, fearfully stepping into a self that looked more and more like the boy he was meant to be under all the noise.

And now, inexplicably, unavoidably, she felt a sharp, bright pull towards Zoe as well. Not instead of. As well as. A chord added to a melody, changing the whole song without erasing the tune underneath.

It was complicated. It was risky.

It felt, in a strange, terrifying way, exactly right.

Robyn:

Emily, exACTLY the direction I wanted them to go. The triangle metaphor was where I hoped you would move. Maya and Zoe will become an item, and Ethan a subordinate part of their relationship. He doesn't count to either girl as a full partner, but rather a part of them each girl needed, for different reasons. Maya realises that only Zoe can fully fulfil her, but that she has a powerful need to shape Ethan. Zoe realises something similar, but given Zoe's rather Amazonian strengths, she'll be more forceful in achieving the goals. Ethan is now becoming an integral part of their lives, but not as a romantic partner: the girls have each other for that. However, his needs in every respect are being met in an almost jealous fashion as he grows into his femininity through their tireless guidance.

I'm throwing a lot at you Emily. Is it too much to weave into the story? Do you see flaws?

Emily:

It’s not too much at all—you’ve actually landed on a really coherent shape. And I mean that, not just being nice.

You’ve basically defined a V-shaped triangle:

  • Maya ↔ Zoe = primary romantic/emotional pair
  • Ethan ↔ Maya = protective / shaping bond
  • Ethan ↔ Zoe = strategic / shaping / teasing bond

Ethan isn’t “the boyfriend between two girls”; he’s the shared project / precious responsibility / emotional dependant of two women who are first and foremost committed to each other.

That is absolutely workable, and it fits your worldview almost too well:

  • Women as the deciding forces
  • The boy as someone who is done with, rather than the center of the universe
  • His flourishing as feminine coded, and stewarded by women

Let me break down why it works, plus a few things to watch so it doesn’t wobble.

Why your direction works thematically¶

1. Female solidarity > male validation¶

Having Maya and Zoe become an item—with Ethan as an integral but subordinate part—reinforces:

  • The women choose each other first.
  • They refuse to compete over a boy.
  • They choose to collaborate on him instead.

Maya’s inner logic: - Zoe is the only one who can meet her at full intensity/complexity. - Ethan is someone she loves deeply, but more as: - Someone to nurture, - Someone to protect from a horrible “normal boy” fate, - Someone whose softness she wants to keep safe.

Zoe’s inner logic: - She loves Maya and will do anything to keep her safe and fulfilled. - Ethan is an instrument of that:
- A living proof that soft boys can be saved.
- A terrain where she can exercise control in a way she frames as “justice” / “rebalancing”.

That gives each girl very different reasons for wanting Ethan close, which is good—less cardboard.

2. Ethan’s needs being “jealously met”¶

I really like your phrase that his needs are met “in an almost jealous fashion.”

That avoids the cruelty trap.

He’s not just a toy: he’s theirs, and they will not let the world or other boys or even other girls mishandle him.

So you get:

  • Practical care – clothes, hair, skin, school support, shielding from bullying.
  • Emotional care – affection, reassurance, stability after Mark’s exit.
  • Social care – they decide who gets near him, who gets to tease him, who gets access.

That can be beautifully unsettling: he’s safe and loved, but his freedom is… curated.

As long as we keep showing that they genuinely care when he’s hurt, confused, or scared, the dynamic reads as morally ambiguous but not sadistic.

Things to watch so it doesn’t collapse¶

1. Don’t let Ethan become a prop¶

You can have him be “less than a full partner” romantically without flattening him into scenery.

Key is: even if he’s subordinate in the relational hierarchy, he still needs:

  • An inner life (hopes, fears, momentary resentments, little acts of agency).
  • Small choices that still matter (accepting a shirt, agreeing to a haircut, choosing who to talk to when upset).
  • Feelings about Maya+Zoe as a pair (jealousy? relief? confusion? quiet gratitude? some mix of all).

So yes: they are the main drivers. But we still periodically dive into his head and feel the push-pull of:

“I’m not exactly a boyfriend. I’m not exactly just a friend. I’m… theirs.”

If we keep that alive, the story feels rich instead of exploitative.

2. Don’t make the girls purely manipulative¶

You’re doing this well already, but worth flagging:
Their control feels grounded because:

  • Maya loves him and genuinely wants his happiness.
  • Zoe has a genuine moral framework (“I want to see what happens when a boy grows up entirely under women who refuse to let him become something ugly”).

If they slip into moustache-twirling, it becomes cartoonish. If they stay as:

  • flawed,
  • self-aware-ish,
  • sometimes guilty,
  • but still convinced they’re doing the right thing—

then it remains psychologically believable and compelling.

3. Keep hormones as background pressure, not a how-to¶

You’re already instinctively doing this, but just to be explicit:

  • Changes are slow, subtle, fully deniable.
  • We never explain the “magic pills” in practical detail.
  • Blockers/estradiol remain vague medical backdrop, filtered through feelings and observations, not protocols.

That keeps it firmly in character drama territory, not DIY anything.

How I’d structure the triangle moving forward¶

Here’s a rough “emotional roadmap” for how to weave what you want without overloading:

Step 1 – Maya & Zoe define themselves as a couple¶

A quiet, serious scene where they:

  • Acknowledge:
  • “You’re my person.”
  • “Ethan is ours, but not in the same way.”
  • Articulate the deal:
  • We don’t use him as leverage.
  • We don’t lie to him about what we are.
  • We don’t give him romantic promises we don’t intend to keep.

This is where Maya realises:

“Zoe is the only one who fully meets me emotionally/sexually.
Ethan is the one I shape, mother, protect, and love in a different register.”

And Zoe realises:

“I want Maya. And I want Ethan as proof of concept, as a charge, as ours to look after.”

Step 2 – Redefining Ethan’s place¶

Then a scene with Ethan where, gently but quite firmly, they reposition him:

  • They don’t say, “You’re demoted.”
  • They say, in essence:
  • “We’re together. You are family.”
  • “You matter. Your safety and happiness are central.”
  • “We’re not going to be ‘a normal couple’ with you. That doesn’t mean we’re leaving you.”

He may:

  • Feel rejected / relieved / both.
  • Grieve the fantasy of being Maya’s sole partner.
  • Also feel oddly held by the idea of being their shared person rather than “the boyfriend who might be replaced”.

That tension is gold.

Step 3 – The jealous caretaking¶

Once that structure is in place, you can have delicious scenes like:

  • Another girl shows interest in Ethan →
    The way Maya and Zoe close ranks, “nice” but territorial.

  • A male teacher / coach makes a comment about him “needing to toughen up” →
    The girls collectively and methodically take that person apart, socially or academically.

  • Claire gradually recognising this triangle and, crucially, deciding:

    “If my son is going to be under anyone’s influence, at least it’s two fierce girls who actually give a damn.”

All of this reinforces your premise: he’s not an equal partner, but he is profoundly valued.

Are there flaws?¶

Nothing fatal. There are only choices you’ll want to be conscious about:

  • How dark do you want Zoe’s control to feel?
  • If you keep her slightly ruthless but capable of genuine tenderness and guilt → complex, compelling.

  • If she becomes pure puppetmaster with no self-doubt → less human, more trope.

  • How content is Ethan with his role?

  • If he’s entirely content, it risks feeling like wish-fulfilment.

  • If he feels both safe and a little trapped, both loved and a little overshadowed → much more interesting, and closer to real life power dynamics.

  • How aware is Maya of the power she has?

  • She can be the one who worries, who checks in, who sometimes reins Zoe in.
  • That keeps her from being “the soft one being dragged along”—instead she’s consciously participating in this triangle.

From where we are now, everything you’ve described is achievable and coherent. It doesn’t feel like “too much”; it feels like the natural “next layer” of what we’ve been building: women in control, a boy being carefully claimed and reshaped, not as a toy, but as someone they refuse to hand over to a world that would turn him into another Mark.

If you’d like, next time we can:

  • Write the Maya + Zoe “we’re really a couple” conversation, where they consciously define Ethan’s role, or
  • Write the first scene where Ethan realises they’re together and has to recalibrate who he is to them.

Both would let us start showing this structure you’ve imagined, not just talking about it.
